<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection_in_amphibians" title="Sexual selection in amphibians">Sexual selection in amphibians</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Orange-thighed frogs in amplexus" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg/220px-Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg/330px-Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg/440px-Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Litoria_xanthomera_amplexus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <a href="/wiki/Orange-thighed_frog" title="Orange-thighed frog">orange-thighed frog</a> (<i>Litoria xanthomera</i>) grasping the female during <a href="/wiki/Amplexus" title="Amplexus">amplexus</a></div></div></div>
<p>For the purpose of <a href="/wiki/Biological_reproduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological reproduction">reproduction</a> most amphibians require <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> although some lay their eggs on land and have developed various means of keeping them moist. A few (e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Fejervarya_raja" class="mw-redirect" title="Fejervarya raja">Fejervarya raja</a></i>) can inhabit brackish water, but there are no true <a href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> amphibians.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> There are reports, however, of particular amphibian populations unexpectedly invading marine waters. Such was the case with the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> invasion of the natural hybrid <i><a href="/wiki/Pelophylax_esculentus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelophylax esculentus">Pelophylax esculentus</a></i> reported in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several hundred frog species in <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiations</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Eleutherodactylus" title="Eleutherodactylus">Eleutherodactylus</a></i>, the Pacific <i><a href="/wiki/Platymantis" title="Platymantis">Platymantis</a></i>, the Australo-Papuan <a href="/wiki/Microhylid" class="mw-redirect" title="Microhylid">microhylids</a>, and many other tropical frogs), however, do not need any water for <a href="/wiki/Breeding_in_the_wild" title="Breeding in the wild">breeding in the wild</a>. They reproduce via direct development, an ecological and evolutionary adaptation that has allowed them to be completely independent from free-standing water. Almost all of these frogs live in wet <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">tropical rainforests</a> and their eggs hatch directly into miniature versions of the adult, passing through the <a href="/wiki/Tadpole" title="Tadpole">tadpole</a> stage within the egg. Reproductive success of many amphibians is dependent not only on the quantity of rainfall, but the seasonal timing.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the tropics, many amphibians breed continuously or at any time of year. In temperate regions, breeding is mostly seasonal, usually in the spring, and is triggered by increasing day length, rising temperatures or rainfall. Experiments have shown the importance of temperature, but the trigger event, especially in arid regions, is often a storm. In anurans, males usually arrive at the breeding sites before females and the vocal chorus they produce may stimulate ovulation in females and the endocrine activity of males that are not yet reproductively active.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995140–141_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995140–141-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In caecilians, fertilisation is internal, the male extruding an <a href="/wiki/Intromittent_organ" title="Intromittent organ">intromittent organ</a>, the phallodeum, and inserting it into the female cloaca. The paired Müllerian glands inside the male cloaca secrete a fluid which resembles that produced by mammalian <a href="/wiki/Prostate" title="Prostate">prostate</a> glands and which may transport and nourish the sperm. Fertilisation probably takes place in the oviduct.<sup id="cite_ref-Duellman_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duellman-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of salamanders also engage in internal fertilisation. In most of these, the male deposits a spermatophore, a small packet of sperm on top of a gelatinous cone, on the <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrate</a> either on land or in the water. The female takes up the sperm packet by grasping it with the lips of the cloaca and pushing it into the vent. The spermatozoa move to the spermatheca in the roof of the cloaca where they remain until ovulation which may be many months later. Courtship rituals and methods of transfer of the spermatophore vary between species. In some, the spermatophore may be placed directly into the female cloaca while in others, the female may be guided to the spermatophore or restrained with an embrace called <a href="/wiki/Amplexus" title="Amplexus">amplexus</a>. Certain primitive salamanders in the families Sirenidae, <a href="/wiki/Hynobiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Hynobiidae">Hynobiidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryptobranchidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptobranchidae">Cryptobranchidae</a> practice external fertilisation in a similar manner to frogs, with the female laying the eggs in water and the male releasing sperm onto the egg mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Duellman_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duellman-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a few exceptions, frogs use external fertilisation. The male grasps the female tightly with his forelimbs either behind the arms or in front of the back legs, or in the case of <i><a href="/wiki/Epipedobates_tricolor" class="mw-redirect" title="Epipedobates tricolor">Epipedobates tricolor</a></i>, around the neck. They remain in amplexus with their cloacae positioned close together while the female lays the eggs and the male covers them with sperm. Roughened nuptial pads on the male's hands aid in retaining grip. Often the male collects and retains the egg mass, forming a sort of basket with the hind feet. An exception is the <a href="/wiki/Granular_poison_frog" title="Granular poison frog">granular poison frog</a> (<i>Oophaga granulifera</i>) where the male and female place their cloacae in close proximity while facing in opposite directions and then release eggs and sperm simultaneously. The <a href="/wiki/Tailed_frog" title="Tailed frog">tailed frog</a> (<i>Ascaphus truei</i>) exhibits internal fertilisation. The "tail" is only possessed by the male and is an extension of the cloaca and used to inseminate the female. This frog lives in fast-flowing streams and internal fertilisation prevents the sperm from being washed away before fertilisation occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995154–162_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995154–162-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The sperm may be retained in storage tubes attached to the <a href="/wiki/Oviduct" title="Oviduct">oviduct</a> until the following spring.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most frogs can be classified as either prolonged or explosive breeders. Typically, prolonged breeders congregate at a breeding site, the males usually arriving first, calling and setting up territories. Other satellite males remain quietly nearby, waiting for their opportunity to take over a territory. The females arrive sporadically, mate selection takes place and eggs are laid. The females depart and territories may change hands. More females appear and in due course, the breeding season comes to an end. Explosive breeders on the other hand are found where temporary pools appear in dry regions after rainfall. These frogs are typically <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fossorial" class="extiw" title="wikt:fossorial">fossorial</a> species that emerge after heavy rains and congregate at a breeding site. They are attracted there by the calling of the first male to find a suitable place, perhaps a pool that forms in the same place each rainy season. The assembled frogs may call in unison and frenzied activity ensues, the males scrambling to mate with the usually smaller number of females.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995154–162_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995154–162-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plethodon_cinereus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plethodon_cinereus.jpg/240px-Plethodon_cinereus.jpg" width="240" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plethodon_cinereus.jpg/360px-Plethodon_cinereus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plethodon_cinereus.jpg/480px-Plethodon_cinereus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2393" data-file-height="1465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plethodon_cinereus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sexual selection has been studied in the <a href="/wiki/Red_back_salamander" class="mw-redirect" title="Red back salamander">red back salamander</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is a direct competition between males to win the attention of the females in salamanders and newts, with elaborate courtship displays to keep the female's attention long enough to get her interested in choosing him to <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mate</a> with.<sup id="cite_ref-romano_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-romano-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Some species store <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> through long breeding seasons, as the extra time may allow for interactions with rival sperm.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span></h2>
<p>Most amphibians go through <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a>, a process of significant morphological change after birth. In typical amphibian development, eggs are laid in water and larvae are adapted to an aquatic lifestyle. Frogs, toads and salamanders all hatch from the egg as larvae with external gills. Metamorphosis in amphibians is regulated by <a href="/wiki/Thyroxine" class="mw-redirect" title="Thyroxine">thyroxine</a> concentration in the blood, which stimulates metamorphosis, and <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">prolactin</a>, which counteracts thyroxine's effect. Specific events are dependent on threshold values for different tissues.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Because most embryonic development is outside the parental body, it is subject to many adaptations due to specific environmental circumstances. For this reason tadpoles can have horny ridges instead of teeth, whisker-like skin extensions or fins. They also make use of a sensory lateral line organ similar to that of fish. After metamorphosis, these organs become redundant and will be reabsorbed by controlled cell death, called <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>. The variety of adaptations to specific environmental circumstances among amphibians is wide, with many discoveries still being made.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs">Eggs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frogspawn_closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Frogspawn" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Frogspawn_closeup.jpg/220px-Frogspawn_closeup.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Frogspawn_closeup.jpg/330px-Frogspawn_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Frogspawn_closeup.jpg/440px-Frogspawn_closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="666" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frogspawn_closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frogspawn, a mass of eggs surrounded by jelly</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg/220px-Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg/330px-Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg/440px-Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amphibian_Egg_Diagram.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amphibian egg:<br /> 1. Jelly capsule 2. Vitelline membrane <br /> 3. Perivitelline fluid 4. <a href="/wiki/Yolk_plug" title="Yolk plug">Yolk plug</a> <br /> 5. Embryo</div></div></div>
<p>The egg of an amphibian is typically surrounded by a transparent gelatinous covering secreted by the oviducts and containing <a href="/wiki/Mucoprotein" title="Mucoprotein">mucoproteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mucopolysaccharide" class="mw-redirect" title="Mucopolysaccharide">mucopolysaccharides</a>. This capsule is permeable to water and gases, and swells considerably as it absorbs water. The ovum is at first rigidly held, but in fertilised eggs the innermost layer liquefies and allows the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> to move freely. This also happens in salamander eggs, even when they are unfertilised. Eggs of some salamanders and frogs contain unicellular green algae. These penetrate the jelly envelope after the eggs are laid and may increase the supply of oxygen to the embryo through photosynthesis. They seem to both speed up the development of the larvae and reduce mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Most eggs contain the pigment <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> which raises their temperature through the absorption of light and also protects them against <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a>. Caecilians, some <a href="/wiki/Plethodontid" class="mw-redirect" title="Plethodontid">plethodontid</a> salamanders and certain frogs lay eggs underground that are unpigmented. In the <a href="/wiki/Wood_frog" title="Wood frog">wood frog</a> (<i>Rana sylvatica</i>), the interior of the globular egg cluster has been found to be up to 6&#160;°C (11&#160;°F) warmer than its surroundings, which is an advantage in its cool northern habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The eggs may be deposited singly or in small groups, or may take the form of spherical egg masses, rafts or long strings. In terrestrial caecilians, the eggs are laid in grape-like clusters in burrows near streams. The amphibious salamander <i><a href="/wiki/Ensatina" title="Ensatina">Ensatina</a></i> attaches its similar clusters by stalks to underwater stems and roots. The <a href="/wiki/Eleutherodactylus_planirostris" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleutherodactylus planirostris">greenhouse frog</a> (<i>Eleutherodactylus planirostris</i>) lays eggs in small groups in the soil where they develop in about two weeks directly into juvenile frogs without an intervening larval stage.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tungara_frog" class="mw-redirect" title="Tungara frog">tungara frog</a> (<i>Physalaemus pustulosus</i>) builds a floating nest from foam to protect its eggs. First a raft is built, then eggs are laid in the centre, and finally a foam cap is overlaid. The foam has anti-microbial properties. It contains no <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a> but is created by whipping up <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lectin" title="Lectin">lectins</a> secreted by the female.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Larvae">Larvae</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif" class="image"><img alt="Frog spawn development" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif/220px-Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif/330px-Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif/440px-Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frog_spawn_time-lapse.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early stages in the development of the embryos of the <a href="/wiki/Common_frog" title="Common frog">common frog</a> (<i>Rana temporaria</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The eggs of amphibians are typically laid in water and hatch into free-living larvae that complete their development in water and later transform into either aquatic or terrestrial adults. In many species of frog and in most lungless salamanders (Plethodontidae), direct development takes place, the larvae growing within the eggs and emerging as miniature adults. Many caecilians and some other amphibians lay their eggs on land, and the newly hatched larvae wriggle or are transported to water bodies. Some caecilians, the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_salamander" title="Alpine salamander">alpine salamander</a> (<i>Salamandra atra</i>) and some of the <a href="/wiki/Nectophrynoides" title="Nectophrynoides">African live-bearing toads</a> (<i>Nectophrynoides spp.</i>) are <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">viviparous</a>. Their larvae feed on glandular secretions and develop within the female's oviduct, often for long periods. Other amphibians, but not caecilians, are <a href="/wiki/Ovoviviparity" title="Ovoviviparity">ovoviviparous</a>. The eggs are retained in or on the parent's body, but the larvae subsist on the yolks of their eggs and receive no nourishment from the adult. The larvae emerge at varying stages of their growth, either before or after metamorphosis, according to their species.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19956–9_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen19956–9-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The toad genus <i>Nectophrynoides</i> exhibits all of these developmental patterns among its dozen or so members.<sup id="cite_ref-species_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-species-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Frogs">Frogs</span></h4>
<p>Frog larvae are known as tadpoles and typically have oval bodies and long, vertically flattened tails with fins. The free-living larvae are normally fully aquatic, but the tadpoles of some species (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nannophrys_ceylonensis" title="Nannophrys ceylonensis">Nannophrys ceylonensis</a></i>) are semi-terrestrial and live among wet rocks.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Tadpoles have cartilaginous skeletons, gills for respiration (external gills at first, internal gills later), <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Lateral line system">lateral line systems</a> and large tails that they use for swimming.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Newly hatched tadpoles soon develop gill pouches that cover the gills. The lungs develop early and are used as accessory breathing organs, the tadpoles rising to the water surface to gulp air. Some species complete their development inside the egg and hatch directly into small frogs. These larvae do not have gills but instead have specialised areas of skin through which respiration takes place. While tadpoles do not have true teeth, in most species, the jaws have long, parallel rows of small keratinized structures called keradonts surrounded by a horny beak.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995179–181_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995179–181-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Front legs are formed under the gill sac and hind legs become visible a few days later.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG/220px-Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG/330px-Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG 2x" data-file-width="379" data-file-height="202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amphibian_Metamorphosis.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amphibian metamorphosis</div></div></div>
<p>Iodine and T4 (over stimulate the spectacular <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> [programmed cell death] of the cells of the larval gills, tail and fins) also stimulate the <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_nervous_systems" title="Evolution of nervous systems">evolution of nervous systems</a> transforming the aquatic, vegetarian tadpole into the terrestrial, carnivorous frog with better neurological, visuospatial, olfactory and cognitive abilities for hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, tadpoles developing in ponds and streams are typically <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a>. Pond tadpoles tend to have deep bodies, large caudal fins and small mouths; they swim in the quiet waters feeding on growing or loose fragments of vegetation. Stream dwellers mostly have larger mouths, shallow bodies and caudal fins; they attach themselves to plants and stones and feed on the surface films of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-EBAnura_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBAnura-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> They also feed on <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a>, filtered from the water through the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>, and stir up the sediment at bottom of the pond, ingesting edible fragments. They have a relatively long, spiral-shaped gut to enable them to digest this diet.<sup id="cite_ref-EBAnura_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBAnura-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Some species are carnivorous at the tadpole stage, eating insects, smaller tadpoles and fish. Young of the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_tree_frog" title="Cuban tree frog">Cuban tree frog</a> (<i>Osteopilus septentrionalis</i>) can occasionally be <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">cannibalistic</a>, the younger tadpoles attacking a larger, more developed tadpole when it is undergoing metamorphosis.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">
<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufobufotadpoles.jpg" class="image" title="Successive stages in the development of common toad (Bufo bufo) tadpoles, finishing with metamorphosis"><img alt="Metamorphosis" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Bufobufotadpoles.jpg/800px-Bufobufotadpoles.jpg" width="800" height="47" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Bufobufotadpoles.jpg/1200px-Bufobufotadpoles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Bufobufotadpoles.jpg/1600px-Bufobufotadpoles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4321" data-file-height="253" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufobufotadpoles.jpg" title="File:Bufobufotadpoles.jpg"> </a></div>Successive stages in the development of <a href="/wiki/Common_toad" title="Common toad">common toad</a> (<i>Bufo bufo</i>) tadpoles, finishing with metamorphosis</div>
</div></div>
<p>At metamorphosis, rapid changes in the body take place as the lifestyle of the frog changes completely. The spiral‐shaped mouth with horny tooth ridges is reabsorbed together with the spiral gut. The animal develops a large jaw, and its gills disappear along with its gill sac. Eyes and legs grow quickly, and a tongue is formed. There are associated changes in the neural networks such as development of stereoscopic vision and loss of the lateral line system. All this can happen in about a day. A few days later, the tail is reabsorbed, due to the higher thyroxine concentration required for this to take place.<sup id="cite_ref-EBAnura_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBAnura-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Salamanders">Salamanders</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Larva of the long-toed salamander" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG/220px-Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG/330px-Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG/440px-Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambystoma_macrodactylum_26597.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Larva of the <a href="/wiki/Long-toed_salamander" title="Long-toed salamander">long-toed salamander</a> <br />(<i>Ambystoma macrodactylum</i>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Larvae of the alpine newt" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg/220px-Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg/330px-Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg/440px-Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="366" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mesotriton_alpestris_apuanus04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Larvae of the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_newt" title="Alpine newt">alpine newt</a> <br />(<i>Ichthyosaura alpestris</i>)</div></div></div>
<p>At hatching, a typical salamander larva has eyes without lids, teeth in both upper and lower jaws, three pairs of feathery external gills, a somewhat laterally flattened body and a long tail with <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Anatomical terms of location">dorsal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Anatomical terms of location">ventral</a> fins. The forelimbs may be partially developed and the hind limbs are rudimentary in pond-living species but may be rather more developed in species that reproduce in moving water. Pond-type larvae often have a pair of balancers, rod-like structures on either side of the head that may prevent the gills from becoming clogged up with sediment. Some members of the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Ambystoma" class="mw-redirect" title="Ambystoma">Ambystoma</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dicamptodon" class="mw-redirect" title="Dicamptodon">Dicamptodon</a></i> have larvae that never fully develop into the adult form, but this varies with species and with populations. The <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_salamander" title="Northwestern salamander">northwestern salamander</a> (<i>Ambystoma gracile</i>) is one of these and, depending on environmental factors, either remains permanently in the larval state, a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">neoteny</a>, or transforms into an adult.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Both of these are able to breed.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Neoteny occurs when the animal's growth rate is very low and is usually linked to adverse conditions such as low water temperatures that may change the response of the tissues to the hormone thyroxine.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Other factors that may inhibit metamorphosis include lack of food, lack of trace elements and competition from <a href="/wiki/Conspecific" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecific">conspecifics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tiger_salamander" title="Tiger salamander">tiger salamander</a> (<i>Ambystoma tigrinum</i>) also sometimes behaves in this way and may grow particularly large in the process. The adult tiger salamander is terrestrial, but the larva is aquatic and able to breed while still in the larval state. When conditions are particularly inhospitable on land, larval breeding may allow continuation of a population that would otherwise die out. There are fifteen species of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/obligate" class="extiw" title="wikt:obligate">obligate</a> neotenic salamanders, including species of <i><a href="/wiki/Necturus" title="Necturus">Necturus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Olm" title="Olm">Proteus</a></i> and <i>Amphiuma</i>, and many examples of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/facultative" class="extiw" title="wikt:facultative">facultative</a> ones that adopt this strategy under appropriate environmental circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lungless salamanders in the family Plethodontidae are terrestrial and lay a small number of unpigmented eggs in a cluster among damp leaf litter. Each egg has a large yolk sac and the larva feeds on this while it develops inside the egg, emerging fully formed as a juvenile salamander. The female salamander often broods the eggs. In the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ensatinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Ensatinas">Ensatinas</a></i>, the female has been observed to coil around them and press her throat area against them, effectively massaging them with a mucous secretion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995196_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995196-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In newts and salamanders, metamorphosis is less dramatic than in frogs. This is because the larvae are already carnivorous and continue to feed as predators when they are adults so few changes are needed to their digestive systems. Their lungs are functional early, but the larvae do not make as much use of them as do tadpoles. Their gills are never covered by gill sacs and are reabsorbed just before the animals leave the water. Other changes include the reduction in size or loss of tail fins, the closure of gill slits, thickening of the skin, the development of eyelids, and certain changes in dentition and tongue structure. Salamanders are at their most vulnerable at metamorphosis as swimming speeds are reduced and transforming tails are encumbrances on land.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Adult salamanders often have an aquatic phase in spring and summer, and a land phase in winter. For adaptation to a water phase, prolactin is the required hormone, and for adaptation to the land phase, thyroxine. External gills do not return in subsequent aquatic phases because these are completely absorbed upon leaving the water for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caecilians">Caecilians</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CaecilianNHM.png" class="image"><img alt="Ichthyophis glutinosus" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/CaecilianNHM.png/220px-CaecilianNHM.png" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/CaecilianNHM.png/330px-CaecilianNHM.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/CaecilianNHM.png/440px-CaecilianNHM.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="507" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CaecilianNHM.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The caecilian <i>Ichthyophis glutinosus</i> with eggs and developing embryo</div></div></div>
<p>Most terrestrial caecilians that lay eggs do so in burrows or moist places on land near bodies of water. The development of the young of <i><a href="/wiki/Ichthyophis_glutinosus" title="Ichthyophis glutinosus">Ichthyophis glutinosus</a></i>, a species from Sri Lanka, has been much studied. The eel-like larvae hatch out of the eggs and make their way to water. They have three pairs of external red feathery gills, a blunt head with two rudimentary eyes, a lateral line system and a short tail with fins. They swim by undulating their body from side to side. They are mostly active at night, soon lose their gills and make sorties onto land. Metamorphosis is gradual. By the age of about ten months they have developed a pointed head with sensory tentacles near the mouth and lost their eyes, lateral line systems and tails. The skin thickens, embedded scales develop and the body divides into segments. By this time, the caecilian has constructed a burrow and is living on land.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siphonops_annulatus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Ringed caecilian" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Siphonops_annulatus.jpg/220px-Siphonops_annulatus.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Siphonops_annulatus.jpg/330px-Siphonops_annulatus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Siphonops_annulatus.jpg/440px-Siphonops_annulatus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="770" data-file-height="482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siphonops_annulatus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Siphonops_annulatus" title="Siphonops annulatus">ringed caecilian</a> (<i>Siphonops annulatus</i>) resembles an earthworm</div></div></div>
<p>In the majority of species of caecilians, the young are produced by viviparity. <i><a href="/wiki/Typhlonectes_compressicauda" class="mw-redirect" title="Typhlonectes compressicauda">Typhlonectes compressicauda</a></i>, a species from South America, is typical of these. Up to nine larvae can develop in the oviduct at any one time. They are elongated and have paired sac-like gills, small eyes and specialised scraping teeth. At first, they feed on the yolks of the eggs, but as this source of nourishment declines they begin to rasp at the ciliated epithelial cells that line the oviduct. This stimulates the secretion of fluids rich in <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> and mucoproteins on which they feed along with scrapings from the oviduct wall. They may increase their length sixfold and be two-fifths as long as their mother before being born. By this time they have undergone metamorphosis, lost their eyes and gills, developed a thicker skin and mouth tentacles, and reabsorbed their teeth. A permanent set of teeth grow through soon after birth.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ringed caecilian (<i><a href="/wiki/Siphonops_annulatus" title="Siphonops annulatus">Siphonops annulatus</a></i>) has developed a unique adaptation for the purposes of reproduction. The progeny feed on a skin layer that is specially developed by the adult in a phenomenon known as maternal dermatophagy. The brood feed as a batch for about seven minutes at intervals of approximately three days which gives the skin an opportunity to regenerate. Meanwhile, they have been observed to ingest fluid exuded from the maternal cloaca.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_care">Parental care</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Common rocket frog" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg/220px-Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg/330px-Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg/440px-Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Panama_Poison_Dart_Frog_Colostethus_panamensis_with_tadpoles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <a href="/wiki/Common_rocket_frog" title="Common rocket frog">common rocket frog</a> (<i>Colostethus panamensis</i>) carrying tadpoles on his back</div></div></div>
<p>The care of offspring among amphibians has been little studied but, in general, the larger the number of eggs in a batch, the less likely it is that any degree of parental care takes place. Nevertheless, it is estimated that in up to 20% of amphibian species, one or both adults play some role in the care of the young.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Those species that breed in smaller water bodies or other specialised habitats tend to have complex patterns of behaviour in the care of their young.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown1_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many woodland salamanders lay clutches of eggs under dead logs or stones on land. The <a href="/wiki/Black_mountain_salamander" title="Black mountain salamander">black mountain salamander</a> (<i>Desmognathus welteri</i>) does this, the mother brooding the eggs and guarding them from predation as the embryos feed on the yolks of their eggs. When fully developed, they break their way out of the egg capsules and disperse as juvenile salamanders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991853–854_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoritWalkerBarnes1991853–854-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The male hellbender, a primitive salamander, excavates an underwater nest and encourages females to lay there. The male then guards the site for the two or three months before the eggs hatch, using body undulations to fan the eggs and increase their supply of oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-EHSAR_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHSAR-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg/220px-Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg/330px-Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg/440px-Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alytes_obstetricans_almogavarii_-_male_with_eggs_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <a href="/wiki/Common_midwife_toad" title="Common midwife toad">common midwife toad</a> (<i>Alytes obstetricans</i>) carrying eggs</div></div></div>
<p>The male <i><a href="/wiki/Colostethus_subpunctatus" class="mw-redirect" title="Colostethus subpunctatus">Colostethus subpunctatus</a></i>, a tiny frog, protects the egg cluster which is hidden under a stone or log. When the eggs hatch, the male transports the tadpoles on his back, stuck there by a mucous secretion, to a temporary pool where he dips himself into the water and the tadpoles drop off.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The male <a href="/wiki/Common_midwife_toad" title="Common midwife toad">midwife toad</a> (<i>Alytes obstetricans</i>) winds egg strings round his thighs and carries the eggs around for up to eight weeks. He keeps them moist and when they are ready to hatch, he visits a pond or ditch and releases the tadpoles.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The female <a href="/wiki/Gastric-brooding_frog" title="Gastric-brooding frog">gastric-brooding frog</a> (<i>Rheobatrachus spp.</i>) reared larvae in her stomach after swallowing either the eggs or hatchlings; however, this stage was never observed before the species became extinct. The tadpoles secrete a hormone that inhibits digestion in the mother whilst they develop by consuming their very large yolk supply.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pouched_frog" title="Pouched frog">pouched frog</a> (<i>Assa darlingtoni</i>) lays eggs on the ground. When they hatch, the male carries the tadpoles around in brood pouches on his hind legs.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The aquatic <a href="/wiki/Surinam_toad" class="mw-redirect" title="Surinam toad">Surinam toad</a> (<i>Pipa pipa</i>) raises its young in pores on its back where they remain until metamorphosis.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The granular poison frog (<i>Oophaga granulifera</i>) is typical of a number of tree frogs in the poison dart frog family <a href="/wiki/Dendrobatidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Dendrobatidae">Dendrobatidae</a>. Its eggs are laid on the forest floor and when they hatch, the tadpoles are carried one by one on the back of an adult to a suitable water-filled crevice such as the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/axil" class="extiw" title="wikt:axil">axil</a> of a leaf or the <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(botany)" title="Rosette (botany)">rosette</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Bromeliaceae" title="Bromeliaceae">bromeliad</a>. The female visits the nursery sites regularly and deposits unfertilised eggs in the water and these are consumed by the tadpoles.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding_and_diet">Feeding and diet</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Northwestern salamander" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg/220px-Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg/330px-Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg/440px-Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1315" data-file-height="1043" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacific_brown_salamander_eating_a_worm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Northwestern_salamander" title="Northwestern salamander">Northwestern salamander</a> <br />(<i>Ambystoma gracile</i>) eating a worm</div></div></div>
<p>With a few exceptions, adult amphibians are <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>, feeding on virtually anything that moves that they can swallow. The diet mostly consists of small prey that do not move too fast such as beetles, caterpillars, earthworms and spiders. The sirens (<i><a href="/wiki/Siren_(genus)" title="Siren (genus)">Siren spp.</a></i>) often ingest aquatic plant material with the invertebrates on which they feed<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and a Brazilian tree frog (<i><a href="/wiki/Xenohyla_truncata" title="Xenohyla truncata">Xenohyla truncata</a></i>) includes a large quantity of fruit in its diet.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_burrowing_toad" title="Mexican burrowing toad">Mexican burrowing toad</a> (<i>Rhinophrynus dorsalis</i>) has a specially adapted tongue for picking up ants and termites. It projects it with the tip foremost whereas other frogs flick out the rear part first, their tongues being hinged at the front.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Food is mostly selected by sight, even in conditions of dim light. Movement of the prey triggers a feeding response. Frogs have been caught on fish hooks baited with red flannel and <a href="/wiki/Rana_clamitans" class="mw-redirect" title="Rana clamitans">green frogs</a> (<i>Rana clamitans</i>) have been found with stomachs full of elm seeds that they had seen floating past.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Toads, salamanders and caecilians also use smell to detect prey. This response is mostly secondary because salamanders have been observed to remain stationary near odoriferous prey but only feed if it moves. Cave-dwelling amphibians normally hunt by smell. Some salamanders seem to have learned to recognize immobile prey when it has no smell, even in complete darkness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199556_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199556-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amphibians usually swallow food whole but may chew it lightly first to subdue it.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> They typically have small hinged <a href="/wiki/Pedicellate_teeth" title="Pedicellate teeth">pedicellate teeth</a>, a feature unique to amphibians. The base and crown of these are composed of <a href="/wiki/Dentine" class="mw-redirect" title="Dentine">dentine</a> separated by an <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">uncalcified</a> layer and they are replaced at intervals. Salamanders, caecilians and some frogs have one or two rows of teeth in both jaws, but some frogs (<i>Rana spp.</i>) lack teeth in the lower jaw, and toads (<i>Bufo spp.</i>) have no teeth. In many amphibians there are also <a href="/wiki/Vomerine_teeth" class="mw-redirect" title="Vomerine teeth">vomerine teeth</a> attached to a facial bone in the roof of the mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199557–58_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199557–58-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Edible frog eating a fellow edible frog" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG/220px-Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG/330px-Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG/440px-Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1184" data-file-height="603" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Teichfrosch_isst_teichfrosch.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edible_frog" title="Edible frog">Edible frog</a> (<i>Pelophylax esculentus</i>) exhibiting <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">cannibalism</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tiger_salamander" title="Tiger salamander">tiger salamander</a> (<i>Ambystoma tigrinum</i>) is typical of the frogs and salamanders that hide under cover ready to ambush unwary invertebrates. Others amphibians, such as the <i>Bufo spp.</i> toads, actively search for prey, while the <a href="/wiki/Argentine_horned_frog" title="Argentine horned frog">Argentine horned frog</a> (<i>Ceratophrys ornata</i>) lures inquisitive prey closer by raising its hind feet over its back and vibrating its yellow toes.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Among leaf litter frogs in Panama, frogs that actively hunt prey have narrow mouths and are slim, often brightly coloured and toxic, while ambushers have wide mouths and are broad and well-camouflaged.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Caecilians do not flick their tongues, but catch their prey by grabbing it with their slightly backward-pointing teeth. The struggles of the prey and further jaw movements work it inwards and the caecilian usually retreats into its burrow. The subdued prey is gulped down whole.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When they are newly hatched, frog larvae feed on the yolk of the egg. When this is exhausted some move on to feed on bacteria, algal crusts, detritus and raspings from submerged plants. Water is drawn in through their mouths, which are usually at the bottom of their heads, and passes through branchial food traps between their mouths and their gills where fine particles are trapped in mucus and filtered out. Others have specialised mouthparts consisting of a horny beak edged by several rows of labial teeth. They scrape and bite food of many kinds as well as stirring up the bottom sediment, filtering out larger particles with the papillae around their mouths. Some, such as the spadefoot toads, have strong biting jaws and are carnivorous or even cannibalistic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995181–185_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995181–185-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="101.70485260771" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Sophisticated-Communication-in-the-Brazilian-Torrent-Frog-Hylodes-japi-pone.0145444.s001.oga" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Sophisticated-Communication-in-the-Brazilian-Torrent-Frog-Hylodes-japi-pone.0145444.s001.oga" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (73 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="73271" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fc/Sophisticated-Communication-in-the-Brazilian-Torrent-Frog-Hylodes-japi-pone.0145444.s001.oga/Sophisticated-Communication-in-the-Brazilian-Torrent-Frog-Hylodes-japi-pone.0145444.s001.oga.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="137992" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sophisticated-Communication-in-the-Brazilian-Torrent-Frog-Hylodes-japi-pone.0145444.s001.oga" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Audio showing Brazilian torrent frog males executing advertisement, peep, and squeal calls.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalization">Vocalization</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_(Cope,_1886).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Male treefrog calling" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg/220px-Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg/330px-Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg/440px-Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_%28Cope%2C_1886%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dendropsophus_microcephalus_-_calling_male_(Cope,_1886).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male treefrog (<i><a href="/wiki/Dendropsophus_microcephalus" title="Dendropsophus microcephalus">Dendropsophus microcephalus</a></i>) inflating his air sac as he calls</div></div></div>
<p>The calls made by caecilians and salamanders are limited to occasional soft squeaks, grunts or hisses and have not been much studied. A clicking sound sometimes produced by caecilians may be a means of orientation, as in bats, or a form of communication. Most salamanders are considered voiceless, but the <a href="/wiki/California_giant_salamander" title="California giant salamander">California giant salamander</a> (<i>Dicamptodon ensatus</i>) has vocal cords and can produce a rattling or barking sound. Some species of salamander emit a quiet squeak or yelp if attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199576–77_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199576–77-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Toad_singing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/American_Toad_singing.jpg/220px-American_Toad_singing.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/American_Toad_singing.jpg/330px-American_Toad_singing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/American_Toad_singing.jpg/440px-American_Toad_singing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="503" data-file-height="409" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Toad_singing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American toad, (<i><a href="/wiki/Anaxyrus_americanus" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaxyrus americanus">Anaxyrus americanus</a></i>) singing</div></div></div>
<p>Frogs are much more vocal, especially during the breeding season when they use their voices to attract mates. The presence of a particular species in an area may be more easily discerned by its characteristic call than by a fleeting glimpse of the animal itself. In most species, the sound is produced by expelling air from the lungs over the vocal cords into an <a href="/wiki/Gular_skin" title="Gular skin">air sac</a> or sacs in the throat or at the corner of the mouth. This may distend like a balloon and acts as a resonator, helping to transfer the sound to the atmosphere, or the water at times when the animal is submerged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199576–77_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen199576–77-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The main vocalisation is the male's loud advertisement call which seeks to both encourage a female to approach and discourage other males from intruding on its territory. This call is modified to a quieter courtship call on the approach of a female or to a more aggressive version if a male intruder draws near. Calling carries the risk of attracting predators and involves the expenditure of much energy.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Other calls include those given by a female in response to the advertisement call and a release call given by a male or female during unwanted attempts at amplexus. When a frog is attacked, a distress or fright call is emitted, often resembling a scream.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The usually nocturnal Cuban tree frog (<i>Osteopilus septentrionalis</i>) produces a rain call when there is rainfall during daylight hours.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_behaviour">Territorial behaviour</span></h2>
<p>Little is known of the territorial behaviour of caecilians, but some frogs and salamanders defend home ranges. These are usually feeding, breeding or sheltering sites. Males normally exhibit such behaviour though in some species, females and even juveniles are also involved. Although in many frog species, females are larger than males, this is not the case in most species where males are actively involved in territorial defence. Some of these have specific adaptations such as enlarged teeth for biting or spines on the chest, arms or thumbs.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Redback_salamander.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Red-backed salamander" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Redback_salamander.jpg/220px-Redback_salamander.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Redback_salamander.jpg/330px-Redback_salamander.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Redback_salamander.jpg/440px-Redback_salamander.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2385" data-file-height="1602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Redback_salamander.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red back salamander (<i><a href="/wiki/Plethodon_cinereus" class="mw-redirect" title="Plethodon cinereus">Plethodon cinereus</a></i>) defends a territory against intruders.</div></div></div>
<p>In salamanders, defence of a territory involves adopting an aggressive posture and if necessary attacking the intruder. This may involve snapping, chasing and sometimes biting, occasionally causing the loss of a tail. The behaviour of <a href="/wiki/Red_back_salamander" class="mw-redirect" title="Red back salamander">red back salamanders</a> (<i>Plethodon cinereus</i>) has been much studied. 91% of marked individuals that were later recaptured were within a metre (yard) of their original daytime retreat under a log or rock.<sup id="cite_ref-Gergits_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gergits-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> A similar proportion, when moved experimentally a distance of 30 metres (98&#160;ft), found their way back to their home base.<sup id="cite_ref-Gergits_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gergits-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The salamanders left odour marks around their territories which averaged 0.16 to 0.33 square metres (1.7 to 3.6&#160;sq&#160;ft) in size and were sometimes inhabited by a male and female pair.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> These deterred the intrusion of others and delineated the boundaries between neighbouring areas. Much of their behaviour seemed stereotyped and did not involve any actual contact between individuals. An aggressive posture involved raising the body off the ground and glaring at the opponent who often turned away submissively. If the intruder persisted, a biting lunge was usually launched at either the tail region or the naso-labial grooves. Damage to either of these areas can reduce the fitness of the rival, either because of the need to regenerate tissue or because it impairs its ability to detect food.<sup id="cite_ref-Gergits_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gergits-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In frogs, male territorial behaviour is often observed at breeding locations; calling is both an announcement of ownership of part of this resource and an advertisement call to potential mates. In general, a deeper voice represents a heavier and more powerful individual, and this may be sufficient to prevent intrusion by smaller males. Much energy is used in the vocalization and it takes a toll on the territory holder who may be displaced by a fitter rival if he tires. There is a tendency for males to tolerate the holders of neighbouring territories while vigorously attacking unknown intruders. Holders of territories have a "home advantage" and usually come off better in an encounter between two similar-sized frogs. If threats are insufficient, chest to chest tussles may take place. Fighting methods include pushing and shoving, deflating the opponent's vocal sac, seizing him by the head, jumping on his back, biting, chasing, splashing, and ducking him under the water.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence_mechanisms">Defence mechanisms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adult_Cane_toad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cane toad" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Adult_Cane_toad.jpg/220px-Adult_Cane_toad.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Adult_Cane_toad.jpg/330px-Adult_Cane_toad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Adult_Cane_toad.jpg/440px-Adult_Cane_toad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4031" data-file-height="2687" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adult_Cane_toad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cane_toad" title="Cane toad">Cane toad</a> (<i>Rhinella marina</i>) with poison glands behind the eyes</div></div></div>
<p>Amphibians have soft bodies with thin skins, and lack claws, defensive armour, or spines. Nevertheless, they have evolved various defence mechanisms to keep themselves alive. The first line of defence in salamanders and frogs is the mucous secretion that they produce. This keeps their skin moist and makes them slippery and difficult to grip. The secretion is often sticky and distasteful or toxic.<sup id="cite_ref-Barthalmus_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barthalmus-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Snakes have been observed yawning and gaping when trying to swallow <a href="/wiki/African_clawed_frog" title="African clawed frog">African clawed frogs</a> (<i>Xenopus laevis</i>), which gives the frogs an opportunity to escape.<sup id="cite_ref-Barthalmus_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barthalmus-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Caecilians have been little studied in this respect, but the Cayenne caecilian (<i>Typhlonectes compressicauda</i>) produces toxic mucus that has killed predatory fish in a feeding experiment in Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> In some salamanders, the skin is poisonous. The <a href="/wiki/Rough-skinned_newt" title="Rough-skinned newt">rough-skinned newt</a> (<i>Taricha granulosa</i>) from North America and other members of its genus contain the neurotoxin <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin</a> (TTX), the most toxic non-protein substance known and almost identical to that produced by <a href="/wiki/Pufferfish" class="mw-redirect" title="Pufferfish">pufferfish</a>. Handling the newts does not cause harm, but ingestion of even the most minute amounts of the skin is deadly. In feeding trials, fish, frogs, reptiles, birds and mammals were all found to be susceptible.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hanifin_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanifin-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The only predators with some tolerance to the poison are certain populations of <a href="/wiki/Common_Garter_Snake" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Garter Snake">common garter snake</a> (<i>Thamnophis sirtalis</i>).
In locations where both snake and salamander co-exist, the snakes have developed immunity through genetic changes and they feed on the amphibians with impunity.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">Coevolution</a> occurs with the newt increasing its toxic capabilities at the same rate as the snake further develops its immunity.<sup id="cite_ref-Hanifin_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanifin-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Some frogs and toads are toxic, the main poison glands being at the side of the neck and under the warts on the back. These regions are presented to the attacking animal and their secretions may be foul-tasting or cause various physical or neurological symptoms. Altogether, over 200 toxins have been isolated from the limited number of amphibian species that have been investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995110_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995110-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salamander-olympus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Fire salamander" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Salamander-olympus.jpg/220px-Salamander-olympus.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Salamander-olympus.jpg/330px-Salamander-olympus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Salamander-olympus.jpg/440px-Salamander-olympus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1569" data-file-height="883" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salamander-olympus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fire_salamander" title="Fire salamander">fire salamander</a> (<i>Salamandra salamandra</i>), a toxic species, wears warning colours.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg/220px-Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg/330px-Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg/440px-Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="895" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Schrecklicherpfeilgiftfrosch-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perhaps the most poisonous animal in the world, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_poison_frog" title="Golden poison frog">golden poison frog</a> (<i>Phyllobates terribilis</i>) is endemic to <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Poisonous species often use bright colouring to warn potential predators of their toxicity. These warning colours tend to be red or yellow combined with black, with the <a href="/wiki/Fire_salamander" title="Fire salamander">fire salamander</a> (<i>Salamandra salamandra</i>) being an example. Once a predator has sampled one of these, it is likely to remember the colouration next time it encounters a similar animal. In some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Fire-bellied_toad" title="Fire-bellied toad">fire-bellied toad</a> (<i>Bombina spp.</i>), the warning colouration is on the belly and these animals adopt a defensive pose when attacked, exhibiting their bright colours to the predator. The frog <i><a href="/wiki/Allobates_zaparo" title="Allobates zaparo">Allobates zaparo</a></i> is not poisonous, but <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">mimics</a> the appearance of other toxic species in its locality, a strategy that may deceive predators.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many amphibians are nocturnal and hide during the day, thereby avoiding diurnal predators that hunt by sight. Other amphibians use <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a> to avoid being detected. They have various colourings such as mottled browns, greys and olives to blend into the background. Some salamanders adopt defensive poses when faced by a potential predator such as the North American <a href="/wiki/Northern_short-tailed_shrew" title="Northern short-tailed shrew">northern short-tailed shrew</a> (<i>Blarina brevicauda</i>). Their bodies writhe and they raise and lash their tails which makes it difficult for the predator to avoid contact with their poison-producing granular glands.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> A few salamanders will autotomise their tails when attacked, sacrificing this part of their anatomy to enable them to escape. The tail may have a constriction at its base to allow it to be easily detached. The tail is regenerated later, but the energy cost to the animal of replacing it is significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Beneski_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beneski-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
Some frogs and toads inflate themselves to make themselves look large and fierce, and some spadefoot toads (<i><a href="/wiki/Pelobates" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelobates">Pelobates</a> spp</i>) scream and leap towards the attacker.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Giant salamanders of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Andrias" title="Andrias">Andrias</a></i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Ceratophryinae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceratophryinae">Ceratophrine</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pyxicephalus" title="Pyxicephalus">Pyxicephalus</a></i> frogs possess sharp teeth and are capable of drawing blood with a defensive bite. The <a href="/wiki/Blackbelly_salamander" title="Blackbelly salamander">blackbelly salamander</a> (<i>Desmognathus quadramaculatus</i>) can bite an attacking common garter snake (<i>Thamnophis sirtalis</i>) two or three times its size on the head and often manages to escape.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognition">Cognition</span></h2>
<p>In amphibians, there is evidence of <a href="/wiki/Habituation" title="Habituation">habituation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Associative_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Associative learning">associative learning</a> through both <a href="/wiki/Classical_conditioning" title="Classical conditioning">classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Instrumental learning">instrumental learning</a>, and discrimination abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In one experiment, when offered live fruit flies (<i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a> virilis</i>), salamanders choose the larger of 1 vs 2 and 2 vs 3. Frogs can distinguish between low numbers (1 vs 2, 2 vs 3, but not 3 vs 4) and large numbers (3 vs 6, 4 vs 8, but not 4 vs 6) of prey. This is irrespective of other characteristics, i.e. surface area, volume, weight and movement, although discrimination among large numbers may be based on surface area.<sup id="cite_ref-Stancher_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stancher-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">Decline in amphibian populations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Golden toad" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bufo_periglenes1.jpg/220px-Bufo_periglenes1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bufo_periglenes1.jpg/330px-Bufo_periglenes1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Bufo_periglenes1.jpg/440px-Bufo_periglenes1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3576" data-file-height="2385" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extinct <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> (<i>Bufo periglenes</i>), last seen in 1989</div></div></div>
<p>Dramatic declines in amphibian populations, including population crashes and mass localized <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, have been noted since the late 1980s from locations all over the world, and amphibian declines are thus perceived to be one of the most critical threats to global <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) reported stating that currently birds,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> mammals, and amphibians extinction rates were at minimum 48 times greater than natural extinction rates—possibly 1,024 times higher.
In 2006 there were believed to be 4,035 species of amphibians that depended on water at some stage during their life cycle. Of these, 1,356 (33.6%) were considered to be threatened and this figure is likely to be an underestimate because it excludes 1,427 species for which there was insufficient data to assess their status.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> A number of causes are believed to be involved, including <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a> and modification, <a href="/wiki/Over-exploitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-exploitation">over-exploitation</a>, pollution, <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, endocrine-disrupting pollutants, destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> (ultraviolet radiation has shown to be especially damaging to the skin, eyes, and eggs of amphibians), and diseases like <a href="/wiki/Chytridiomycosis" title="Chytridiomycosis">chytridiomycosis</a>. However, many of the causes of amphibian declines are still poorly understood, and are a topic of ongoing discussion.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Israel_painted_frog.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Hula painted frog" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Israel_painted_frog.JPG/220px-Israel_painted_frog.JPG" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Israel_painted_frog.JPG/330px-Israel_painted_frog.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Israel_painted_frog.JPG/440px-Israel_painted_frog.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1073" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Israel_painted_frog.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hula_painted_frog" title="Hula painted frog">Hula painted frog</a> (<i>Discoglossus nigriventer</i>) was believed to be extinct but was rediscovered in 2011.</div></div></div>
<p>With their complex reproductive needs and permeable skins, amphibians are often considered to be <a href="/wiki/Ecological_indicator" title="Ecological indicator">ecological indicators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Waddle_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waddle-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In many terrestrial ecosystems, they constitute one of the largest parts of the vertebrate biomass. Any decline in amphibian numbers will affect the patterns of predation. The loss of carnivorous species near the top of the food chain will upset the delicate ecosystem balance and may cause dramatic increases in opportunistic species. In the Middle East, a growing appetite for eating frog legs and the consequent gathering of them for food was linked to an increase in <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitoes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Predators that feed on amphibians are affected by their decline. The <a href="/wiki/Western_terrestrial_garter_snake" title="Western terrestrial garter snake">western terrestrial garter snake</a> (<i>Thamnophis elegans</i>) in California is largely aquatic and depends heavily on two species of frog that are decreasing in numbers, the <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_toad" title="Yosemite toad">Yosemite toad</a> (<i>Bufo canorus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_yellow-legged_frog" title="Mountain yellow-legged frog">mountain yellow-legged frog</a> (<i>Rana muscosa</i>), putting the snake's future at risk. If the snake were to become scarce, this would affect birds of prey and other predators that feed on it.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, in the ponds and lakes, fewer frogs means fewer tadpoles. These normally play an important role in controlling the growth of algae and also forage on <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a> that accumulates as sediment on the bottom. A reduction in the number of tadpoles may lead to an overgrowth of algae, resulting in depletion of oxygen in the water when the algae later die and decompose. Aquatic invertebrates and fish might then die and there would be unpredictable ecological consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995249_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStebbinsCohen1995249-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Global_strategy" title="Global strategy">global strategy</a> to stem the crisis was released in 2005 in the form of the Amphibian Conservation Action Plan. Developed by over eighty leading experts in the field, this call to action details what would be required to curtail amphibian declines and extinctions over the following five years and how much this would cost. The Amphibian Specialist Group of the IUCN is spearheading efforts to implement a comprehensive global strategy for amphibian conservation.<sup id="cite_ref-AmphibianArk_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmphibianArk-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amphibian_Ark" title="Amphibian Ark">Amphibian Ark</a> is an organization that was formed to implement the ex-situ conservation recommendations of this plan, and they have been working with zoos and aquaria around the world, encouraging them to create assurance colonies of threatened amphibians.<sup id="cite_ref-AmphibianArk_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmphibianArk-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> One such project is the Panama Amphibian Rescue and Conservation Project that built on existing conservation efforts in Panama to create a country-wide response to the threat of chytridiomycosis.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Roček, Z. (2000). "14. Mesozoic Amphibians".
